			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Ivy League</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the group of colleges and the athletic conference that gave the group its name. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Ivy League (disambiguation)">Ivy League (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>Ivy League</b> is a collegiate <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCAA_conferences" title="List of NCAA conferences">athletic conference</a> comprising sports teams from eight private institutions of <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">higher education</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern United States</a>. The conference name is also commonly used to refer to those eight schools as a group beyond the sports context.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The eight institutions are <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>. The term <i>Ivy League</i> has connotations of academic excellence, selectivity in admissions, and social <a href="/wiki/Elitism" title="Elitism">elitism</a>.</p>
<p>While the term was in use as early as 1933, it became official only after the formation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">NCAA</a> <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division&#160;I</a> athletic conference in 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-officialhistory_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-officialhistory-3">[3]</a></sup> Seven of the eight schools were founded during the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">United States colonial period</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a> was founded in 1865. Ivy League institutions account for seven of the nine <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Colleges" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonial Colleges">Colonial Colleges</a> chartered before the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>; the other two are <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a>.</p>
<p>Ivy League schools are generally viewed as some of the most prestigious, and are ranked among the best universities worldwide by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-World.27s_Best_Colleges_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World.27s_Best_Colleges-4">[4]</a></sup> All eight universities place in the top fifteen of the <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> 2017 <a href="/wiki/College_and_university_rankings" title="College and university rankings">nation university rankings</a>, including the top four schools and five of the top eight.<sup id="cite_ref-National_University_Rankings_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_University_Rankings-5">[5]</a></sup> U.S. News has named a member of the Ivy League as the best national university in each of the past seventeen years ending with the 2017 rankings: Princeton ten times, Harvard twice, and the two schools tied for first five times.</p>
<p>Undergraduate enrollments range from about 4,000 to 14,000,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> making them larger than those of a typical private <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts_college" title="Liberal arts college">liberal arts college</a> and smaller than a typical public <a href="/wiki/State_university_system" title="State university system">state university</a>. Total enrollments, including graduate students, range from approximately 6,400 at Dartmouth to over 20,000 at Columbia, Cornell, Harvard, and Penn. Ivy League <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">financial endowments</a> range from Brown's $3.2 billion to Harvard's $36.4 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_institutions_of_higher_education_by_endowment" title="Lists of institutions of higher education by endowment">largest financial endowment</a> of any academic institution in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Members">Members</span></h2>
<p>Ivy League universities have some of the largest university <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">financial endowments</a> in the world, which allows the universities to provide many resources for their academic programs and research endeavors. As of 2014, Harvard University has an endowment of $36.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Additionally, each university receives millions of dollars in research grants and other subsidies from federal and state governments.</p>

<dl>
<dd><small><b>Note:</b> Six of the eight Ivy League universities consider their founding dates to be simply the date that they received their charters and thus became legal corporations with the authority to grant academic degrees. Harvard University uses the date that the legislature of the Massachusetts Bay Colony formally allocated funds for the creation of a college. Harvard was chartered in 1650, although classes had been conducted for approximately a decade by then. The University of Pennsylvania initially considered its founding date to be 1750; this is the year which appears on the first iteration of the university seal.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Later in Penn's early history, the university changed its officially recognized founding date to 1749, which was used for all of the nineteenth century, including a centennial celebration in 1849. In 1899, Penn's board of trustees formally adopted a third founding date of 1740, in response to a petition from Penn's General Alumni Society. Penn was chartered in 1755, the same year collegiate classes began. "Religious affiliation" refers to financial sponsorship, formal association with, and promotion by, a religious denomination. All of the schools in the Ivy League are private and not currently associated with any religion.</small></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_name">Origin of the name</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Yard,_Harvard_University.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/220px-Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/330px-Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG/440px-Harvard_Yard%2C_Harvard_University.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG/220px-Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG/330px-Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG/440px-Yale_University_Old_Campus_04.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3306" data-file-height="2025" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_Hall,_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_Hall%2C_Dartmouth_College_-_general_view.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

On the Dartmouth Green, 2007: Dartmouth Hall and Thornton Hall</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Students have long revered the ivied walls of older colleges. "Planting the ivy" was a customary class day ceremony at many colleges in the 1800s. In 1893 an alumnus told <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">The Harvard Crimson</a></i>, "In 1850, class day was placed upon the University Calendar.... the custom of planting the ivy, while the ivy oration was delivered, arose about this time."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> At Penn, graduating seniors started the custom of planting ivy at a university building each spring in 1873 and that practice was formally designated as "<a href="/wiki/Ivy_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Ivy stone">Ivy Day</a>" in 1874.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Ivy planting ceremonies are reported for Yale,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Simmons_College_(Massachusetts)" class="mw-redirect" title="Simmons College (Massachusetts)">Simmons</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr</a><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and many others. Princeton's "Ivy Club" was founded in 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first usage of <i>Ivy</i> in reference to a group of colleges is from sportswriter Stanley Woodward (1895–1965).</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>A proportion of our eastern ivy colleges are meeting little fellows another Saturday before plunging into the strife and the turmoil.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Stanley Woodward, <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Tribune" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Tribune">New York Tribune</a></i>, October 14, 1933, describing the football season<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The first known instance of the term <i>Ivy League</i> being used appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Science_Monitor" title="The Christian Science Monitor">The Christian Science Monitor</a></i> on February 7, 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-officialhistory_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-officialhistory-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Several sportswriters and other journalists used the term shortly later to refer to the older colleges, those along the northeastern seaboard of the United States, chiefly the nine institutions with origins dating from the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_colleges" title="Colonial colleges">colonial era</a>, together with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> (West Point), the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a>, and a few others. These schools were known for their long-standing traditions in intercollegiate athletics, often being the first schools to participate in such activities. However, at this time, none of these institutions made efforts to form an athletic league.</p>
<p>A common <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a> attributes the name to the Roman numeral for four (IV), asserting that there was such a sports league originally with four members. The <i>Morris Dictionary of Word and Phrase Origins</i> helped to perpetuate this belief. The supposed "IV League" was formed over a century ago and consisted of Harvard, Yale, Princeton, and a fourth school that varies depending on who is telling the story.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> However, it is clear that <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College,_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College, Columbia University">Columbia</a> met on November 23, 1876 at the so-called Massasoit Convention to decide on uniform rules for the emerging game of American football, which rapidly spread.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Pre–Ivy_League"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre.E2.80.93Ivy_League">Pre–Ivy League</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG/220px-Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG/330px-Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG/440px-Chancellor_Green_exterior_Princeton.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/220px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/330px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG/440px-Brown_university_robinson_hall_2009a.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>

Robinson Hall at Brown, 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornell_Baseball2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cornell_Baseball2.jpg/100px-Cornell_Baseball2.jpg" width="100" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cornell_Baseball2.jpg/150px-Cornell_Baseball2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cornell_Baseball2.jpg/200px-Cornell_Baseball2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3844" data-file-height="5597" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cornell_Baseball2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cornell baseball player, 1908</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Seven out of the eight Ivy League schools were founded before the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>; Cornell was founded just after the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. These seven were the primary colleges in the Northern and Middle Colonies, and their early faculties and founding boards were largely, therefore, drawn from other Ivy League institutions. There were also some British graduates from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St._Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="University of St. Andrews">University of St. Andrews</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>, and elsewhere on their boards. Similarly, the founder of <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_William_%26_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="The College of William &amp; Mary">The College of William &amp; Mary</a>, in 1693, was a British graduate of the University of Edinburgh. Cornell provided <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> with its <a href="/wiki/David_Starr_Jordan" title="David Starr Jordan">first president</a>.</p>
<p>The influence of these institutions on the founding of other colleges and universities is notable. This included the Southern public college movement which blossomed in the decades surrounding the turn of the 19th century when Georgia, South Carolina, North Carolina and Virginia established what became the flagship universities for each of these states. In 1801, a majority of the first board of trustees for what became the <a href="/wiki/University_of_South_Carolina" title="University of South Carolina">University of South Carolina</a> were Princeton alumni. They appointed <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Maxcy" title="Jonathan Maxcy">Jonathan Maxcy</a>, a Brown graduate, as the university's first president. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cooper_(US_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cooper (US politician)">Thomas Cooper</a>, an Oxford alumnus and University of Pennsylvania faculty member, became the second president of the South Carolina college. The founders of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a> came from Yale, hence the school colors of University of California are <a href="/wiki/Yale_Blue" title="Yale Blue">Yale Blue</a> and California Gold.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the Ivy League schools have identifiable <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> roots, while others were founded as non-sectarian schools. <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> <i>King's College</i> broke up during the Revolution and was reformed as public nonsectarian <a href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Columbia_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia College of Columbia University">Columbia College</a>. In the early nineteenth century, the specific purpose of training Calvinist ministers was handed off to theological seminaries, but a denominational tone and such relics as compulsory chapel often lasted well into the twentieth century. Penn and Brown were officially founded as nonsectarian schools. Brown's charter promised no religious tests and "full liberty of conscience", but placed control in the hands of a board of twenty-two Baptists, five Quakers, four Congregationalists, and five Episcopalians. Cornell has been strongly nonsectarian from its founding.</p>
<p>"Ivy League" is sometimes used as a way of referring to an <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a> <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">class</a>, even though institutions such as Cornell University were among the first in the United States to reject racial and gender discrimination in their admissions policies. This dates back to at least 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Novels<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-45">[45]</a></sup> and memoirs<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> attest this sense, as a social elite; to some degree independent of the actual schools.</p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>, the present Ivy League institutions slowly widened their selection of their students. They had always had distinguished faculties; some of the first Americans with <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorates</a> had taught for them; but they now decided that they could not both be world-class research institutions and be competitive in the highest ranks of American college sport; in addition, the schools experienced the scandals of any other big-time football programs, although more quietly.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_athletic_league">History of the athletic league</span></h3>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_and_early_20th_centuries">19th and early 20th centuries</span></h4>
<p>The first formal athletic league involving eventual Ivy League schools (or any US colleges, for that matter) was created in 1870 with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Rowing_Association_of_American_Colleges" title="Rowing Association of American Colleges">Rowing Association of American Colleges</a>. The RAAC hosted a de facto national championship in rowing during the period 1870–1894. In 1895, Cornell, Columbia, and Penn founded the <a href="/wiki/Intercollegiate_Rowing_Association" title="Intercollegiate Rowing Association">Intercollegiate Rowing Association</a>, which remains the oldest collegiate athletic organizing body in the US. To this day, the IRA Championship Regatta determines the national champion in rowing and all of the Ivies are regularly invited to compete.</p>
<p>A basketball league was later created in 1902, when Columbia, Cornell, Harvard, Yale and Princeton formed the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Intercollegiate_Basketball_League" title="Eastern Intercollegiate Basketball League">Eastern Intercollegiate Basketball League</a>; they were later joined by Penn and Dartmouth.</p>
<p>In 1906, the organization that eventually became the <a href="/wiki/NCAA" class="mw-redirect" title="NCAA">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> was formed, primarily to formalize rules for the emerging sport of football. But of the 39 original member colleges in the NCAA, only two of them (Dartmouth and Penn) later became Ivies.</p>
<p>In February 1903, intercollegiate wrestling began when Yale accepted a challenge from Columbia, published in the Yale News. The dual meet took place prior to a basketball game hosted by Columbia and resulted in a tie. Two years later, Penn and Princeton also added wrestling teams, leading to the formation of the student-run Intercollegiate Wrestling Association, now the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Intercollegiate_Wrestling_Association" title="Eastern Intercollegiate Wrestling Association">Eastern Intercollegiate Wrestling Association</a> (EIWA), the first and oldest collegiate wrestling league in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1930, Columbia, Cornell, Dartmouth, Penn, Princeton and Yale formed the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Intercollegiate_Baseball_League" title="Eastern Intercollegiate Baseball League">Eastern Intercollegiate Baseball League</a>; they were later joined by Harvard, Brown, Army and Navy.</p>
<p>Before the formal establishment of the Ivy League, there was an "unwritten and unspoken agreement among certain Eastern colleges on athletic relations". The earliest reference to the "Ivy colleges" came in 1933, when <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Woodward" title="Stanley Woodward">Stanley Woodward</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald_Tribune" title="New York Herald Tribune">New York Herald Tribune</a> used it to refer to the eight current members plus Army.<sup id="cite_ref-officialhistory_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-officialhistory-3">[3]</a></sup> In 1935, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> reported on an example of collaboration between the schools:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The athletic authorities of the so-called "Ivy League" are considering drastic measures to curb the increasing tendency toward riotous attacks on goal posts and other encroachments by spectators on playing fields.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>The Associated Press, <i>The New York Times</i><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Despite such collaboration, the universities did not seem to consider the formation of the league as imminent. <a href="/wiki/Romeyn_Berry" title="Romeyn Berry">Romeyn Berry</a>, Cornell's manager of athletics, reported the situation in January 1936 as follows:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I can say with certainty that in the last five years—and markedly in the last three months—there has been a strong drift among the eight or ten universities of the East which see a good deal of one another in sport toward a closer bond of confidence and cooperation and toward the formation of a common front against the threat of a breakdown in the ideals of amateur sport in the interests of supposed expediency.</p>
<p>Please do not regard that statement as implying the organization of an Eastern conference or even a poetic "Ivy League". That sort of thing does not seem to be in the cards at the moment.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Within a year of this statement and having held month-long discussions about the proposal, on December 3, 1936, the idea of "the formation of an Ivy League" gained enough traction among the undergraduate bodies of the universities that the <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Daily_Spectator" title="Columbia Daily Spectator">Columbia Daily Spectator</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cornell_Daily_Sun" title="The Cornell Daily Sun">The Cornell Daily Sun</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dartmouth" title="The Dartmouth">The Dartmouth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">The Harvard Crimson</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Pennsylvanian" title="The Daily Pennsylvanian">The Daily Pennsylvanian</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Princetonian" title="The Daily Princetonian">The Daily Princetonian</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Daily_News" title="Yale Daily News">Yale Daily News</a></i> would simultaneously run an editorial entitled "Now Is the Time", encouraging the seven universities to form the league in an effort to preserve the ideals of athletics.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Part of the editorial read as follows:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Ivy League exists already in the minds of a good many of those connected with football, and we fail to see why the seven schools concerned should be satisfied to let it exist as a purely nebulous entity where there are so many practical benefits which would be possible under definite organized association. The seven colleges involved fall naturally together by reason of their common interests and similar general standards and by dint of their established national reputation they are in a particularly advantageous position to assume leadership for the preservation of the ideals of intercollegiate athletics.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Ivies have been competing in sports as long as intercollegiate sports have existed in the United States. Rowing teams from Harvard and Yale met in the first sporting event held between students of two U.S. colleges on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipesaukee" title="Lake Winnipesaukee">Lake Winnipesaukee</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, on August 3, 1852. Harvard's team, "The Oneida", won the race and was presented with trophy black walnut oars from then presidential nominee General <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>. The proposal did not succeed—on January 11, 1937, the athletic authorities at the schools rejected the "possibility of a heptagonal league in football such as these institutions maintain in basketball, baseball and track." However, they noted that the league "has such promising possibilities that it may not be dismissed and must be the subject of further consideration."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Post–World_War_II"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post.E2.80.93World_War_II">Post–World War II</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG/220px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG/330px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG/440px-Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dartmouth_College_campus_2007-11-06_Tuck_School_of_Business.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Students in Stell Hall at Dartmouth's <a href="/wiki/Tuck_School_of_Business" title="Tuck School of Business">Tuck School of Business</a>, 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg/220px-Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg/330px-Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg/440px-Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="284" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scroll_and_Key_1866_delegation_Yale_College.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Students of Yale College, 1866</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1945 the presidents of the eight schools signed the first <i>Ivy Group Agreement</i>, which set academic, financial, and athletic standards for the <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> teams. The principles established reiterated those put forward in the Harvard-Yale-Princeton Presidents' Agreement of 1916. The Ivy Group Agreement established the core tenet that an applicant's ability to play on a team would not influence admissions decisions:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The members of the Group reaffirm their prohibition of athletic scholarships. Athletes shall be admitted as students and awarded financial aid only on the basis of the same academic standards and economic need as are applied to all other students.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1954, the presidents extended the Ivy Group Agreement to all intercollegiate sports, effective with the 1955–56 basketball season. This is generally reckoned as the formal formation of the Ivy League. As part of the transition, Brown, the only Ivy that hadn't joined the EIBL, did so for the 1954–55 season. A year later, the Ivy League absorbed the EIBL. The Ivy League claims the EIBL's history as its own. Through the EIBL, it is the oldest basketball conference in Division I.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>As late as the 1960s many of the Ivy League universities' undergraduate programs remained open only to men, with Cornell the only one to have been coeducational from its founding (1865) and Columbia being the last (1983) to become <a href="/wiki/Coeducation" class="mw-redirect" title="Coeducation">coeducational</a>. Before they became coeducational, many of the Ivy schools maintained extensive social ties with nearby <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(colleges)" title="Seven Sisters (colleges)">Seven Sisters</a> <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_college" title="Women's college">women's colleges</a>, including weekend visits, dances and parties inviting Ivy and Seven Sisters students to mingle. This was the case not only at <a href="/wiki/Barnard_College" title="Barnard College">Barnard College</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, which are adjacent to Columbia and Harvard, but at more distant institutions as well. The movie <i><a href="/wiki/Animal_House" title="Animal House">Animal House</a></i> includes a satiric version of the formerly common visits by Dartmouth men to Massachusetts to meet <a href="/wiki/Smith_College" title="Smith College">Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Holyoke_College" title="Mount Holyoke College">Mount Holyoke</a> women, a drive of more than two hours. As noted by Irene Harwarth, Mindi Maline, and Elizabeth DeBra, "The '<a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(colleges)" title="Seven Sisters (colleges)">Seven Sisters'</a> was the name given to Barnard, Smith, Mount Holyoke, <a href="/wiki/Vassar_College" title="Vassar College">Vassar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wellesley_College" title="Wellesley College">Wellesley</a>, and Radcliffe, because of their parallel to the Ivy League men’s colleges."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1982 the Ivy League considered adding two members, with Army, Navy, and <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern</a> as the most likely candidates; if it had done so, the league could probably have avoided being moved into the recently created Division I-AA (now Division I FCS) for football.<sup id="cite_ref-white19820110_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white19820110-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1983, following the admission of women to Columbia College, Columbia University and Barnard College entered into an athletic consortium agreement by which students from both schools compete together on Columbia University women's athletic teams, which replaced the women's teams previously sponsored by Barnard.</p>
<p>When Army and Navy departed the Eastern Intercollegiate Baseball League in 1992, all intercollegiate competition involving the eight schools became united under the Ivy League banner.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span></h2>

<p>The Ivy League schools are highly selective, with acceptance rates since 2000 ranging from 6 to 16 percent at each of the universities. Admitted students come from around the world, although students from <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern United States</a> make up a significant proportion of students.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prestige">Prestige</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation</a></div>
<p>Members of the League have been highly ranked by various <a href="/wiki/University_rankings" class="mw-redirect" title="University rankings">university rankings</a>.</p>
<table style="text-align:center; float:left; font-size:85%; margin-right:2em;" class="wikitable">
<caption>National academic rankings</caption>
<tr>
<th>University<br />
<small>(in alphabetical order)</small></th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">ARWU</a><br />
<small>(2014)</small><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></th>

<p>Further, Ivy League members have produced many <a href="/wiki/Nobel_laureates" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel laureates">Nobel laureates</a>, winners of the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences</a>. According to the Nobel Foundation's website, as of 2016 the number of prize-winners affiliated with each Ivy League university at the time of their awards is: Brown, 2; Columbia, 17; Cornell, 8; Dartmouth, 0; Harvard, 36; Penn, 4; Princeton, 14; and Yale, 8.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In addition, each university self-reports their number of affiliated Nobel laureates, but they use varying definitions for which Nobel winners they claim (for example, alumni, active faculty, former faculty, visiting faculty, adjunct faculty, etc.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaboration">Collaboration</span></h3>
<p>Collaboration between the member schools is illustrated by the student-led <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Council" title="Ivy Council">Ivy Council</a> that meets in the fall and spring of each year, with representatives from every Ivy League school. The governing body of the Ivy League is the Council of Ivy Group Presidents, composed of each university president. During meetings, the presidents often discuss common procedures and initiatives for the universities.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fashion_and_lifestyle">Fashion and lifestyle</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League_(clothes)" title="Ivy League (clothes)">Ivy League (clothes)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preppy" title="Preppy">Preppy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Take_Ivy" title="Take Ivy">Take Ivy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League_(haircut)" title="Ivy League (haircut)">Ivy League (haircut)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg/280px-Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg" width="280" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg/420px-Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg/560px-Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Architecture_on_University_of_Pennsylvania_Campus_-_Young_Ben_Franklin_Statue_-_Philadelphia_-_Pennsylvania_-_04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> on the campus of the University of Pennsylvania, 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Different fashion trends and styles have emerged from Ivy League campuses over time, and fashion trends such as <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League_(clothes)" title="Ivy League (clothes)">Ivy League</a> and <a href="/wiki/Preppy" title="Preppy">Preppy</a> are styles often associated with the Ivy League and its culture.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ivy_League_(clothes)" title="Ivy League (clothes)">Ivy League style</a> is a style of men's dress, popular during the late 1950s, believed to have originated on Ivy League campuses. The clothing stores <a href="/wiki/J._Press" title="J. Press">J. Press</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brooks_Brothers" title="Brooks Brothers">Brooks Brothers</a> represent perhaps the quintessential Ivy League dress manner. The Ivy League style is said to be the predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/Preppy" title="Preppy">preppy</a> style of dress.</p>
<p>Preppy fashion started around 1912 to the late 1940s and 1950s as the Ivy League style of dress.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._Press" title="J. Press">J. Press</a> represents the quintessential preppy clothing brand, stemming from the collegiate traditions that shaped the preppy subculture. In the mid-twentieth century J. Press and <a href="/wiki/Brooks_Brothers" title="Brooks Brothers">Brooks Brothers</a>, both being pioneers in preppy fashion, had stores on Ivy League school campuses, including <a href="/wiki/Harvard" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard">Harvard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yale" class="mw-redirect" title="Yale">Yale</a>.</p>
<p>Some typical preppy styles also reflect traditional upper class <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> leisure activities, such as <a href="/wiki/Horse_riding" class="mw-redirect" title="Horse riding">equestrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yacht" title="Yacht">yachting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fencing" title="Fencing">fencing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">rowing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">rugby</a>. Longtime New England outdoor outfitters, such as <a href="/wiki/L.L._Bean" class="mw-redirect" title="L.L. Bean">L.L. Bean</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Zlotnick_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zlotnick-78">[78]</a></sup> became part of conventional preppy style. This can be seen in sport stripes and colours, equestrian clothing, plaid shirts, field jackets and nautical-themed accessories. Vacationing in <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach,_Florida" title="Palm Beach, Florida">Palm Beach, Florida</a>, long popular with the East Coast upper class, led to the emergence of bright colour combinations in leisure wear seen in some brands such as <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Pulitzer" title="Lilly Pulitzer">Lilly Pulitzer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zlotnick_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zlotnick-78">[78]</a></sup> By the 1980s, other brands such as <a href="/wiki/Lacoste" title="Lacoste">Lacoste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Izod" title="Izod">Izod</a><sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_Kellogg_285_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_Kellogg_285-79">[79]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dooney_%26_Bourke" title="Dooney &amp; Bourke">Dooney &amp; Bourke</a> became associated with preppy style.'</p>
<p>Today, these styles continue to be popular on Ivy League campuses, throughout the U.S., and abroad, and are oftentimes labeled as "Classic American style" or "Traditional American style".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_elitism">Social elitism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ADWhiteReadingRoom,_CornellUniversity.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg/220px-ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg/330px-ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg/440px-ADWhiteReadingRoom%2C_CornellUniversity.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ADWhiteReadingRoom,_CornellUniversity.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The A.D. White Reading Room at Cornell's <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University_Library" title="Cornell University Library">Uris Library</a>, 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbiaman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Columbiaman.jpg/170px-Columbiaman.jpg" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Columbiaman.jpg/255px-Columbiaman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Columbiaman.jpg/340px-Columbiaman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="658" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Columbiaman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A cartoon portrait of the stereotypical Columbia man, 1902</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ivy League is often associated with the <a href="/wiki/American_upper_class" title="American upper class">upper class</a> <a href="/wiki/White_Anglo-Saxon_Protestant" title="White Anglo-Saxon Protestant">White Anglo-Saxon Protestant</a> community of the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_money" title="Old money">Old Money</a>, or more generally, the <a href="/wiki/Upper_middle_class_in_the_United_States" title="Upper middle class in the United States">American upper middle</a> and upper classes.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Although most Ivy League students come from upper middle- and upper-class families, the student body has become increasingly more economically and ethnically diverse. The universities provide significant financial aid to help increase the enrollment of lower income and middle class students.<sup id="cite_ref-theatlantic.com_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatlantic.com-86">[86]</a></sup> Several reports suggest, however, that the proportion of students from less-affluent families remains low.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phrases such as "Ivy League snobbery"<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> are ubiquitous in nonfiction and fiction writing of the early and mid-twentieth century. A <a href="/wiki/Louis_Auchincloss" title="Louis Auchincloss">Louis Auchincloss</a> character dreads "the aridity of snobbery which he knew infected the Ivy League colleges".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-45">[45]</a></sup> A business writer, warning in 2001 against discriminatory hiring, presented a cautionary example of an attitude to avoid (the bracketed phrase is his):</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We Ivy Leaguers [read: mostly white and Anglo] know that an Ivy League degree is a mark of the kind of person who is likely to succeed in this organization.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The phrase <i>Ivy League</i> historically has been perceived as connected not only with academic excellence, but also with social elitism. In 1936, sportswriter <a href="/wiki/John_Kieran" title="John Kieran">John Kieran</a> noted that student editors at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">Penn</a> were advocating the formation of an athletic association. In urging them to consider "<a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">Georgetown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fordham_University" title="Fordham University">Fordham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syracuse_University" title="Syracuse University">Syracuse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">Pitt</a>" as candidates for membership, he exhorted:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It would be well for the proponents of the Ivy League to make it clear (to themselves especially) that the proposed group would be inclusive but not "exclusive" as this term is used with a slight up-tilting of the tip of the nose.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Aspects of Ivy stereotyping were illustrated during the 1988 presidential election, when <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (Yale '48) derided <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">Michael Dukakis</a> (graduate of Harvard Law School) for having "foreign-policy views born in Harvard Yard's boutique."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <i>New York Times</i> columnist <a href="/wiki/Maureen_Dowd" title="Maureen Dowd">Maureen Dowd</a> asked "Wasn't this a case of the pot calling the kettle elite?" Bush explained, however, that, unlike Harvard, Yale's reputation was "so diffuse, there isn't a symbol, I don't think, in the Yale situation, any symbolism in it.... Harvard boutique to me has the connotation of liberalism and elitism" and said <i>Harvard</i> in his remark was intended to represent "a philosophical enclave" and not a statement about class.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Columnist <a href="/wiki/Russell_Baker" title="Russell Baker">Russell Baker</a> opined that "Voters inclined to loathe and fear elite Ivy League schools rarely make fine distinctions between Yale and Harvard. All they know is that both are full of rich, fancy, stuck-up and possibly dangerous intellectuals who never sit down to supper in their <a href="/wiki/Undershirt" title="Undershirt">undershirt</a> no matter how hot the weather gets."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Still, the last five presidents have all attended Ivy League schools for at least part of their education— George H. W. Bush (Yale undergrad), <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> (Yale Law School), <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> (Yale undergrad, Harvard Business School), <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> (Columbia undergrad, Harvard Law School), and <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> (Penn undergrad).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._presidents_in_the_Ivy_League">U.S. presidents in the Ivy League</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_education" title="List of Presidents of the United States by education">List of Presidents of the United States by education</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904,_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach,_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg/190px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg" width="190" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg/285px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg/380px-Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904%2C_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach%2C_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2366" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Franklin_D._Roosevelt_with_Harvard_class_of_1904,_group_shot_in_Nantasket_Beach,_Massachusetts_-_NARA_-_195358.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a>, third from left, top row, with his Harvard class in 1904</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Of the forty-four men who have served as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, fifteen have graduated from an Ivy League university. Of them, eight have degrees from Harvard, five from Yale, three from Columbia, two from Princeton and one from Penn. Twelve presidents have earned Ivy undergraduate degrees. Three of these were transfer students: Donald Trump transferred from <a href="/wiki/Fordham_University" title="Fordham University">Fordham University</a>, Barack Obama transferred from Occidental College and John F. Kennedy transferred from another Ivy, Princeton, where he had been class of 1939. <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> was the first president to graduate from college, graduating from Harvard in 1755.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_demographics">Student demographics</span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span></h3>
<p>Students of the Ivy League largely hail from <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">the Northeast</a>, largely from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> areas. As all eight Ivy League universities are within the Northeast, it is no surprise that most graduates end up working and residing in the Northeast after graduation. An unscientific survey of Harvard seniors from the Class of 2013 found that 42% hailed from the Northeast and 55% overall were planning on working and residing in the Northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Boston and New York City are traditionally where many Ivy League graduates end up living.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomics_and_social_class">Socioeconomics and social class</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg/275px-Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg" width="275" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg/413px-Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg/550px-Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg 2x" data-file-width="782" data-file-height="561" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Learned_Hand_at_Harvarda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a> students circa 1895</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Students of the Ivy League, both graduate and undergraduate, come primarily from <a href="/wiki/Upper_middle_class_in_the_United_States" title="Upper middle class in the United States">upper middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_upper_class" title="American upper class">upper class</a> families. In recent years, however, the universities have looked towards increasing socioeconomic and class diversity, by providing greater financial aid packages to applicants from <a href="/wiki/American_lower_class" title="American lower class">lower</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_working_class" class="mw-redirect" title="American working class">working</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_middle_class" title="American middle class">middle class</a> American families.<sup id="cite_ref-theatlantic.com_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatlantic.com-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McGrath_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, 46% of Harvard undergraduate students came from families in the top 3.8% of all American households (i.e., over $200,000 annual income).<sup id="cite_ref-McGrath_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-104">[104]</a></sup> In 2012, the bottom 25% of the American income distribution accounted for only 3–4% of students at Brown, a figure that had remained unchanged since 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In 2014, 69% of incoming freshmen students at Yale College came from families with annual incomes of over $120,000, putting most Yale College students in the upper middle and/or upper class. (The median household income in the U.S. in 2013 was $52,700.)<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 2011–2012 academic year, students qualifying for <a href="/wiki/Pell_Grant" title="Pell Grant">Pell Grants</a> (federally funded scholarships on the basis of need) comprised 20% at Harvard, 18% at Cornell, 17% at Penn, 16% at Columbia, 15% at Dartmouth and Brown, 14% at Yale, and 12% at Princeton. Nationally, 35% of American university students qualify for a Pell Grant.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_and_athletics">Competition and athletics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg/220px-BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg/330px-BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg/440px-BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BrownUniversity-OpeningConvocation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
New students pass through Van Wickle Gates at <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>'s opening Convocation, 2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg/220px-Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg/330px-Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg/440px-Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="548" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yale-Harvard-Game.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bowl" title="Yale Bowl">Yale Bowl</a> in 2001 during the annual football game played between Harvard and Yale</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ivy champions are recognized in sixteen men's and sixteen women's sports. In some sports, Ivy teams actually compete as members of another league, the Ivy championship being decided by isolating the members' records in play against each other; for example, the six league members who participate in <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> do so as members of <a href="/wiki/ECAC_Hockey" title="ECAC Hockey">ECAC Hockey</a>, but an Ivy champion is extrapolated each year. The Ivy League was the last Division I <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> conference to institute a conference postseason tournament; the first tournaments for men and women were held at the end of the 2016–17 season. The tournaments only award the Ivy League automatic bids for the NCAA Division&#160;I <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="NCAA Division I Men's Basketball Tournament">Men's</a> and <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Women%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="NCAA Division I Women's Basketball Tournament">Women's</a> Basketball Tournaments; the official conference championships continue to be awarded based solely on regular-season results.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Before the 2016–17 season, the automatic bids were based solely on regular-season record, with a <a href="/wiki/One-game_playoff" title="One-game playoff">one-game playoff</a> (or series of one-game playoffs if more than two teams were tied) held to determine the automatic bid.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The Ivy League is one of only two Division I conferences which award their official basketball championships solely on regular-season results; the other is the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Since its inception, an Ivy League school has yet to win either the men's or women's Division I NCAA Basketball Tournament.</p>
<p>On average, each Ivy school has more than 35 varsity teams. All eight are in the top 20 for number of sports offered for both men and women among Division I schools. Unlike most Division I athletic conferences, the Ivy League prohibits the granting of athletic scholarships; all scholarships awarded are need-based (<a href="/wiki/Student_financial_aid_(United_States)" title="Student financial aid (United States)">financial aid</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-whatisivy_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatisivy-112">[112]</a></sup> In addition, the Ivies have a rigid policy against <a href="/wiki/Redshirt_(college_sports)" title="Redshirt (college sports)">redshirting</a>, even for medical reasons; an athlete loses a year of eligibility for every year enrolled at an Ivy institution.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Ivy League teams' non-league games are often against the members of the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_League" title="Patriot League">Patriot League</a>, which have similar academic standards and athletic scholarship policies (although unlike the Ivies, the Patriot League allows redshirting).</p>
<p>In the time before <a href="/wiki/College_recruiting" title="College recruiting">recruiting</a> for college sports became dominated by those offering athletic scholarships and lowered academic standards for athletes, the Ivy League was successful in many sports relative to other universities in the country. In particular, Princeton won 26 recognized national championships in <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a> (last in 1935), and Yale won 18 (last in 1927).<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Both of these totals are considerably higher than those of other historically strong programs such as <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Crimson_Tide_football" title="Alabama Crimson Tide football">Alabama</a>, which has won 15, <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Fighting_Irish_football" title="Notre Dame Fighting Irish football">Notre Dame</a>, which claims 11 but is credited by many sources with 13, and <a href="/wiki/USC_Trojans_football" title="USC Trojans football">USC</a>, which has won 11. Yale, whose coach <a href="/wiki/Walter_Camp" title="Walter Camp">Walter Camp</a> was the "Father of American Football," held on to its place as the all-time wins leader in college football throughout the entire 20th century, but was finally passed by <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Wolverines_football" title="Michigan Wolverines football">Michigan</a> on November 10, 2001. Harvard, Yale, Princeton and Penn each have over a dozen former scholar-athletes enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="College Football Hall of Fame">College Football Hall of Fame</a>. Currently Dartmouth holds the record for most Ivy League football titles, with 18, followed closely by Harvard and Penn, each with 17 titles. In addition, the Ivy League has produced <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">Super Bowl</a> winners <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Boothe" title="Kevin Boothe">Kevin Boothe</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cornell_Big_Red_football" title="Cornell Big Red football">Cornell</a>), two-time <a href="/wiki/Pro_Bowl" title="Pro Bowl">Pro Bowler</a> <a href="/wiki/Zak_DeOssie" title="Zak DeOssie">Zak DeOssie</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brown_Bears_football" title="Brown Bears football">Brown</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sean_Morey_(American_football)" title="Sean Morey (American football)">Sean Morey</a> (Brown), <a href="/wiki/All-Pro" title="All-Pro">All-Pro</a> selection <a href="/wiki/Matt_Birk" title="Matt Birk">Matt Birk</a> (<a href="/wiki/Harvard_Crimson_football" title="Harvard Crimson football">Harvard</a>), <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Hill" title="Calvin Hill">Calvin Hill</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yale_Bulldogs_football" title="Yale Bulldogs football">Yale</a>), <a href="/wiki/Derrick_Harmon" title="Derrick Harmon">Derrick Harmon</a> (Cornell) and <a href="/wiki/1999_NFL_draft" class="mw-redirect" title="1999 NFL draft">1999</a> "<a href="/wiki/Mr._Irrelevant" title="Mr. Irrelevant">Mr. Irrelevant</a>" <a href="/wiki/Jim_Finn" title="Jim Finn">Jim Finn</a> (<a href="/wiki/Penn_Quakers_football" title="Penn Quakers football">Penn</a>).</p>
<p>Beginning with the 1982 football season, the Ivy League has competed in Division I-AA (renamed <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I#Football_Championship_Subdivision" title="NCAA Division I">FCS</a> in 2006).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The Ivy League teams are eligible for the FCS tournament held to determine the national champion, and the league champion is eligible for an automatic bid (and any other team may qualify for an at-large selection) from the NCAA. However, the Ivy League has not played any postseason games at all since 1956 due to the league's concerns about the extended December schedule's effects on academics. For this reason, any Ivy League team invited to the FCS playoffs turns down the bid. The Ivy League plays a strict 10-game schedule, compared to other FCS members' schedules of 11 (or, in some seasons, 12) regular season games, plus post-season, which was most recently expanded in <a href="/wiki/2013_NCAA_Division_I_FCS_football_season" title="2013 NCAA Division I FCS football season">2013</a> to five rounds with 24 teams, with a bye week for the top eight teams. Football is the only sport in which the Ivy League declines to compete for a national title.</p>
<p>In addition to varsity football, Penn, Princeton and Cornell also field teams in the eight-team <a href="/wiki/Sprint_football" title="Sprint football">Collegiate Sprint Football League</a>, in which all players must weigh 172 pounds or less. Penn and Princeton are the last remaining founding members of the league from its 1934 debut, and Cornell is the next-oldest, joining in 1937. Yale and Columbia previously fielded teams in the league but no longer do so.</p>

<p>The Ivy League is home to some of the oldest <a href="/wiki/College_rugby" class="mw-redirect" title="College rugby">college rugby</a> teams in the United States. Although these teams are not "varsity" sports, they compete annually in the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Rugby_Conference" title="Ivy Rugby Conference">Ivy Rugby Conference</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_results">Historical results</span></h3>

<p>The table above includes the number of team championships won from the beginning of official Ivy League competition (1956–57 academic year) through 2011–12. Princeton and Harvard have on occasion won ten or more Ivy League titles in a year, an achievement accomplished six times by Harvard and 21 times by Princeton, including a conference-record 15 championships in 2010–11. Only once has one of the other six schools earned more than eight titles in a single academic year (Cornell with nine in 2005–06). In the 33 academic years beginning 1979–80, Princeton has averaged 11 championships per year, one-third of the conference total of 33 sponsored sports.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the seven academic years beginning 2005–06, Harvard has won Ivy titles in 22 different sports, two-thirds of the league total, and Princeton has won championships in 31 different sports, all except wrestling and men's tennis.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivalries">Rivalries</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palestra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Palestra.jpg/220px-Palestra.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Palestra.jpg/330px-Palestra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Palestra.jpg/440px-Palestra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palestra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Built in 1927, Penn's <a href="/wiki/Palestra" title="Palestra">Palestra</a> is shown around 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg/220px-Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg/330px-Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg/440px-Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg 2x" data-file-width="963" data-file-height="679" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harvard_Stadium_-_1903_Greek_Play.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Performance of a Greek play at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Stadium" title="Harvard Stadium">Harvard Stadium</a> in 1903</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rivalries run deep in the Ivy League. For instance, Princeton and <a href="/wiki/Penn_Quakers_men%27s_basketball" title="Penn Quakers men's basketball">Penn</a> are longstanding <a href="/wiki/Penn_%E2%80%93_Princeton_basketball_rivalry" class="mw-redirect" title="Penn – Princeton basketball rivalry">men's basketball rivals</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> "Puck Frinceton" and "Pennetrate the Puss" T-shirts are worn by Quaker fans at games.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In only 11 instances in the history of Ivy League basketball, and in only seven seasons since Yale's 1962 title, has neither Penn nor Princeton won at least a share of the Ivy League title in basketball,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> with Princeton champion or co-champion 26 times and Penn 25 times. Penn has won 21 outright, Princeton 19 outright. Princeton has been a co-champion 7 times, sharing 4 of those titles with Penn (these 4 seasons represent the only times Penn has been co-champion). Harvard won its first title of either variety in 2011, losing a dramatic play-off game to Princeton for the NCAA tournament bid, then rebounded to win outright championships in 2012, 2013, and 2014.</p>
<p>Rivalries exist between other Ivy league teams in other sports, including <a href="/wiki/Cornell-Harvard_hockey_rivalry" class="mw-redirect" title="Cornell-Harvard hockey rivalry">Cornell and Harvard in hockey</a>, Harvard and Princeton in swimming, and Harvard and Penn in football (Penn and Harvard have won 28 Ivy League Football Championships since 1982, Penn-16; Harvard-12). During that time Penn has had 8 undefeated Ivy League Football Championships and Harvard has had 6 undefeated Ivy League Football Championships.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Field_lacrosse" title="Field lacrosse">men's lacrosse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_Big_Red_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Cornell Big Red men's lacrosse">Cornell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Tigers_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Princeton Tigers men's lacrosse">Princeton</a> are perennial rivals, and they are the only two Ivy League teams to have won the NCAA tournament. In 2009, the Big Red and Tigers met for their 70th game in the <a href="/wiki/2009_NCAA_Division_I_Men%27s_Lacrosse_Championship" title="2009 NCAA Division I Men's Lacrosse Championship">NCAA tournament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> No team other than Harvard or Princeton has won the men's swimming conference title outright since 1972, although Yale, Columbia, and Cornell have shared the title with Harvard and Princeton during this time. Similarly, no program other than Princeton and Harvard has won the women's swimming championship since Brown's 1999 title. Princeton or Cornell has won every indoor and outdoor track and field championship, both men's and women's, every year since 2002–03, with one exception (Columbia women won indoor championship in 2012). Harvard and Yale are <a href="/wiki/The_Game_(Harvard-Yale)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Game (Harvard-Yale)">football</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvard-Yale_Regatta" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard-Yale Regatta">crew</a> rivals although the competition has become unbalanced; Harvard has won all but one of the last 15 football games and all but one of the last 13 crew races.</p>
