<p>The Yale-Princeton series is the nation's second longest, exceeded only by <a href="/wiki/The_Rivalry_(Lehigh-Lafayette)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Rivalry (Lehigh-Lafayette)">"The Rivalry"</a> between <a href="/wiki/Lehigh_Mountain_Hawks_football" title="Lehigh Mountain Hawks football">Lehigh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_Leopards_football" title="Lafayette Leopards football">Lafayette</a>, which began later in 1884 but included two or three games in each of 17 early seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> For the first three decades of the Yale-Princeton rivalry, the two played their season-ending game at a neutral site, usually New York City, and with one exception (1890: Harvard), the winner of the game also won at least a share of the <a href="/wiki/College_football_national_championships_in_NCAA_Division_I_FBS" title="College football national championships in NCAA Division I FBS">national championship</a> that year, covering the period 1869 through 1903.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> This phenomenon of a finale contest at a neutral site for the national title created a social occasion for the society elite of the metropolitan area akin to a <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">Super Bowl</a> in the era prior to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a> in 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> These football games were also financially profitable for the two universities, so much that they began to play baseball games in New York City as well, drawing record crowds for that sport also, largely from the same social demographic.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In a period when the only professional sports were fledgling baseball leagues, these high-profile early contests between Princeton and Yale played a role in popularizing spectator sports, demonstrating their financial potential and raising public awareness of Ivy universities at a time when few people attended college.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Ivies">Other Ivies</span></h2>
<p>The term <i>Ivy</i> is often used to connote a positive comparison to or association with the Ivy League, often along academic lines. The term has been used to describe the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ivies" title="Little Ivies">Little Ivies</a>, a grouping of small liberal arts colleges in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> Other uses include the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ivy" title="Southern Ivy">Southern Ivies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hidden_Ivies" title="Hidden Ivies">Hidden Ivies</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivies</a>. The 2007 edition of <i>Newsweek's How to Get Into College Now</i>, the editors designated 25 schools as "New Ivies".<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ivy_Plus">Ivy Plus</span></h3>
<p>The term <i>Ivy Plus</i> is sometimes used to refer to the Ancient Eight plus several other schools for purposes of alumni associations,<sup id="cite_ref-BluePrint_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BluePrint-136">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Untangling_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Untangling-137">[136]</a></sup> university affiliations,<sup id="cite_ref-Untangling_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Untangling-137">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sustain_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sustain-138">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> or endowment comparisons.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DangerousWealth_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DangerousWealth-143">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-144">[143]</a></sup> In his book <i>Untangling the Ivy League</i>, Zawel writes, "The inclusion of non–Ivy League schools under this term is commonplace for some schools and extremely rare for others. Among these other schools, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> are almost always included. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> are often included as well."<sup id="cite_ref-Untangling_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Untangling-137">[136]</a></sup> In their 2015 book <i>Acing Admissions</i>, Mehta and Dixit write, "The [<i>Ivy Plus</i> schools] include, but are not limited to: Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT), Stanford University and <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern University</a>. Besides selectivity, these Ivy Plus colleges are thought to share similar values around academic and professional excellence, intellectual curiosity, leadership and civil engagement."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Platopainting.jpg/23px-Platopainting.jpg" width="23" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Platopainting.jpg/34px-Platopainting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Platopainting.jpg/46px-Platopainting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="400" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:University" title="Portal:University">University portal</a></span></li>
</ul>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Little_Ivies" title="Little Ivies">Little Ivies</a>—a grouping of small liberal arts colleges in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern United States</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(colleges)" title="Seven Sisters (colleges)">Seven Sisters</a>—seven liberal arts colleges all previously open to only enrollment by women considered the Ivy League of female-only colleges</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Little_Three" title="Little Three">Little Three</a>—three private liberal arts colleges in Massachusetts and Connecticut (<a href="/wiki/Amherst_College" title="Amherst College">Amherst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_University" title="Wesleyan University">Wesleyan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Williams_College" title="Williams College">Williams</a>), in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Big_Three_(colleges)" title="Big Three (colleges)">Big Three</a> (a term used to refer to Harvard, Yale, and Princeton) of the Ivy League.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colby-Bates-Bowdoin_Consortium" title="Colby-Bates-Bowdoin Consortium">Colby-Bates-Bowdoin Consortium</a>—three private liberal arts colleges in Maine (<a href="/wiki/Bates_College" title="Bates College">Bates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bowdoin_College" title="Bowdoin College">Bowdoin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colby_College" title="Colby College">Colby</a>) known as the Maine Big Three contrasting also with the Ivy League's Big Three.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Ivy_League_medical_schools" title="List of Ivy League medical schools">List of Ivy League medical schools</a>—schools of the Ivy League universities that offer medical education (both <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">MDs</a> and <a href="/wiki/PhD" class="mw-redirect" title="PhD">PhDs</a>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Ivy_League_law_schools" title="List of Ivy League law schools">List of Ivy League law schools</a>—schools of the Ivy League universities that offer various law degrees.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Ivy_League_business_schools" title="List of Ivy League business schools">List of Ivy League business schools</a>—schools of the Ivy League universities that offer various business degrees, especially the <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Business_Administration" title="Master of Business Administration">MBA</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hidden_Ivies" title="Hidden Ivies">Hidden Ivies: Thirty Colleges of Excellence</a></i>—complementary use of <i>Ivy</i> to characterize schools in the United States that showcase academic excellence.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jesuit_Ivy" title="Jesuit Ivy">Jesuit Ivy</a>—complementary use of <i>Ivy</i> to characterize <a href="/wiki/Boston_College" title="Boston College">Boston College</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Black_Ivy_League" title="Black Ivy League">Black Ivy League</a>—informal list of private historically black colleges that attracted a high number of top African American students.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/SKY_(universities)" title="SKY (universities)">SKY (universities)</a> A group of most prestigious Korean universities</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.upenn.edu/about/facts.php">"Penn: Penn Facts"</a>. The University of Pennsylvania<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">8 October</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=Penn%3A+Penn+Facts&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=The+University+of+Pennsylvania&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.upenn.edu%2Fabout%2Ffacts.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Adrian Rodrigues, "Yale endowment rises to all-time high," <i>Yale Daily News</i>, Sept. 25, 2014 (yaledailynews.com/blog/2014/09/25/yale-endowment-rises-to-all-time-high)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.yale.edu/about/facts.html">"About – Facts"</a>. Yale University<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">8 October</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=About+%E2%80%93+Facts&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=Yale+University&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.yale.edu%2Fabout%2Ffacts.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The institution, though founded in 1636, did not receive its name until 1639. It was nameless for its first two years</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-PennFoundingYear-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-PennFoundingYear_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a> for details of the circumstances of Penn's origin. Penn considered its founding date to be 1749 for over a century.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.archives.upenn.edu/histy/features/1700s/trustees.html">[1]</a> In 1895, elite universities in the United States agreed that henceforth formal <a href="/wiki/Academic_procession" title="Academic procession">academic processions</a> would place visiting dignitaries and other officials in the order of their institution's founding dates. Penn's periodical "The Alumni Register," published by the General Alumni Society, then began a grassroots campaign to retroactively revise the university's founding date to 1740, in order to appear older than <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, which had been chartered in 1746. In 1899, the Board of Trustees acceded to the alumni initiative and voted to change the founding date to 1740. The rationale offered in 1899 was that, in 1750, founder Benjamin Franklin and his original board of trustees purchased a completed but unused building and assumed an unnamed trust from a group which had hoped to begin a church and charity school in Philadelphia. This edifice was commonly called the "New Building" by local citizens and was referred to by such name in Franklin's memoirs as well as the legal bill of sale in Penn's archives. No name is stated or known for the associated educational trust, hence "Unnamed Charity School" serves as a placeholder to refer to the trust which is the premise for Penn's association with a founding date of 1740. The first named entity in Penn's early history was the 1751 secondary school for boys and charity school for indigent children called "Academy and Charitable School in the Province of Pennsylvania."<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.upenn.edu/about/heritage.php">[2]</a> Undergraduate education began in 1755 and the organization then changed its name to "College, Academy and Charity School of Philadelphia in Pennsylvania."<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.archives.upenn.edu/histy/features/1700s/penn1700s.html">[3]</a> Operation of the charity school was discontinued a few years later.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Penn's website, like other sources, makes an important point of Penn's heritage being nonsectarian, associated with <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> and the Academy of Philadelphia's nonsectarian board of trustees: "The goal of Franklin's nonsectarian, practical plan would be the education of a business and governing class rather than of clergymen."<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.archives.upenn.edu/histy/features/1700s/penn1700s.html">[4]</a>. Jencks and Riesman (2001) write "The Anglicans who founded the University of Pennsylvania, however, were evidently anxious not to alienate Philadelphia's Quakers, and they made their new college officially nonsectarian." In Franklin's 1749 founding <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.archives.upenn.edu/primdocs/1749proposals.html">Proposals relating to the education of youth in Pensilvania</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dewey.library.upenn.edu/sceti/printedbooksNew/index.cfm?TextID=franklin_youth&amp;PagePosition=20">(page images)</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071018223123/http://dewey.library.upenn.edu/sceti/printedbooksNew/index.cfm?TextID=franklin_youth&amp;PagePosition=20">Archived</a> 2007-10-18 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>., religion is not mentioned directly as a subject of study, but he states in a footnote that the study of "<i>History</i> will also afford frequent Opportunities of showing the Necessity of a <i>Publick Religion,</i> from its Usefulness to the Publicks; the Advantage of a Religious Character among private Persons; the Mischiefs of Superstition, &amp;c. and the Excellency of the CHRISTIAN RELIGION above all others antient or modern." Starting in 1751, the same trustees also operated a Charity School for Boys, whose curriculum combined "general principles of Christianity" with practical instruction leading toward careers in business and the "mechanical arts." <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.archives.upenn.edu/histy/features/1700s/charitysch.html">[5]</a>, and thus might be described as "non-denominational Christian." The charity school was originally planned and a trust was organized on paper in 1740 by followers of travelling evangelist <a href="/wiki/George_Whitefield" title="George Whitefield">George Whitefield</a>. The school was to have operated inside a church supported by the same group of adherents. But the organizers ran short of financing and, although the frame of the building was raised, the interior was left unfinished. The founders of the Academy of Philadelphia purchased the unused building in 1750 for their new venture and, in the process, assumed the original trust. Since 1899, Penn has claimed a founding date of 1740, based on the organizational date of the charity school and the premise that it had institutional identity with the Academy of Philadelphia. Whitefield was a firebrand Methodist associated with <a href="/wiki/Great_Awakening" title="Great Awakening">The Great Awakening</a>; since the Methodists did not formally break from the Church of England until 1784, Whitefield in 1740 would be labelled <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Episcopalian</a>, and in fact <i>Brown</i> University, emphasizing its own pioneering nonsectarianism, refers to Penn's origin as "Episcopalian".<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://www.brown.edu/Administration/Admission/gettoknowus/ourhistory.html">[6]</a> Penn is sometimes assumed to have Quaker ties (its athletic teams are called "Quakers," and the cross-registration alliance between Penn, Haverford, Swarthmore and Bryn Mawr is known as the "Quaker Consortium.") But Penn's website does not assert any formal affiliation with Quakerism, historic or otherwise, and <a href="/wiki/Haverford_College" title="Haverford College">Haverford College</a> implicitly asserts a non-Quaker origin for Penn when it states that "Founded in 1833, Haverford is the oldest institution of higher learning with Quaker roots in North America."<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120204054925/https://www.haverford.edu/publicrelations/news/QandA.html">"Archived copy"</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.haverford.edu/publicrelations/news/QandA.html">the original</a> on 2012-02-04<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2012-02-19</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.haverford.edu%2Fpublicrelations%2Fnews%2FQandA.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101229090817/http://www.1911encyclopedia.org/Protestant_Episcopal_Church">"Protestant Episcopal Church – LoveToKnow 1911"</a>. 1911encyclopedia.org. 2006-10-06. <li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.brown.edu/Administration/Admission/gettoknowus/ourhistory.html">"Brown Admission: Our History"</a>. Brown.edu<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-01-30</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=Brown+Admission%3A+Our+History&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=Brown.edu&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.brown.edu%2FAdministration%2FAdmission%2Fgettoknowus%2Fourhistory.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-princetonchapeltour-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-princetonchapeltour_27-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-princetonchapeltour_27-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.princeton.edu/~oktour/virtualtour/english/Stop05.htm">"University Chapel: Orange Key Virtual Tour of Princeton University"</a>. Princeton University.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=University+Chapel%3A+Orange+Key+Virtual+Tour+of+Princeton+University&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=Princeton+University&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.princeton.edu%2F~oktour%2Fvirtualtour%2Fenglish%2FStop05.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Brown's website characterizes it as "the Baptist answer to Congregationalist Yale and Harvard; Presbyterian Princeton; and Episcopalian Penn and Columbia," but adds that at the time it was "the only one that welcomed students of all religious persuasions."<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://www.brown.edu/Administration/Admission/gettoknowus/ourhistory.html">[7]</a> Brown's charter stated that "into this liberal and catholic institution shall never be admitted any religious tests, but on the contrary, all the members hereof shall forever enjoy full, free, absolute, and uninterrupted liberty of conscience." The charter called for twenty-two of the thirty-six trustees to be Baptists, but required that the remainder be "five Friends, four Congregationalists, and five Episcopalians."<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://encyclopedia.jrank.org/PRE_PYR/PROVIDENCE.html">[8]</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hughes, Samuel (2002). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.upenn.edu/gazette/0102/0102finals.html">"Whiskey, Loose Women, and Fig Leaves: The University's seal has a curious history"</a>. <i>Pennsylvania Gazette</i>. <b>100</b> (3).</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.atitle=Whiskey%2C+Loose+Women%2C+and+Fig+Leaves%3A+The+University%27s+seal+has+a+curious+history&amp;rft.aufirst=Samuel&amp;rft.aulast=Hughes&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Pennsylvania+Gazette&amp;rft.volume=100&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.upenn.edu%2Fgazette%2F0102%2F0102finals.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.upenn.edu/pennnews/current/2002/101702/askbenny.html">"The Penn Current / October 17, 2002 / Ask Benny"</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2011-01-30</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.btitle=The+Penn+Current+%2F+October+17%2C+2002+%2F+Ask+Benny&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=Upenn.edu&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.upenn.edu%2Fpennnews%2Fcurrent%2F2002%2F101702%2Faskbenny.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Kieran, John (December 4, 1936). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://query.nytimes.com/gst/abstract.html?res=9C0CE3D9173EEE3BBC4C53DFB467838D629EDE&amp;legacy=true">"Sports of the Times — The Ivy League"</a>. <i>The New York Times</i>. p.&#160;36<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">May 30,</span> 2017</span>. <q>There will now be a little test of 'the power of the press' in intercollegiate circles since the student editors at Harvard, Yale, Princeton, Cornell, Columbia, Dartmouth and Penn are coming out in a group for the formation of an Ivy League in football. The idea isn't new.... It would be well for the proponents of the Ivy League to make it clear (to themselves especially) that the proposed group would be inclusive but not 'exclusive' as this term is used with a slight up-tilting of the tip of the nose." He recommended the consideration of "plenty of institutions covered with home-grown ivy that are not included in the proposed group. [such as ] Army and Navy and Georgetown and Fordham and Syracuse and Brown and Pitt, just to offer a few examples that come to mind" and noted that "Pitt and Georgetown and Brown and Bowdoin and Rutgers were old when Cornell was shining new, and Fordham and Holy Cross had some building draped in ivy before the plaster was dry in the walls that now tower high about Cayuga's waters.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.atitle=Sports+of+the+Times+%E2%80%94+The+Ivy+League&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft.aulast=Kieran&amp;rft.date=1936-12-04&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.pages=36&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fquery.nytimes.com%2Fgst%2Fabstract.html%3Fres%3D9C0CE3D9173EEE3BBC4C53DFB467838D629EDE%26legacy%3Dtrue&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

(October 5, 2016). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.espn.com/blog/collegebasketballnation/post/_/id/116147/which-players-injured-last-season-will-make-the-strongest-comebacks">"Which players injured last season will make the strongest comebacks?"</a>. <i>ESPN.com</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">October 8,</span> 2016</span>. <q>It's easy to forget what Siyani Chambers has meant to Harvard as a three-time all-Ivy League player because he wasn't enrolled in school last season. The Ivy League doesn't allow redshirts, so Chambers was forced to withdraw after a preseason ACL injury if he wanted to return for his senior season.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIvy+League&amp;rft.aufirst=C.L.&amp;rft.aulast=Brown&amp;rft.btitle=Which+players+injured+last+season+will+make+the+strongest+comebacks%3F&amp;rft.date=2016-10-05&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=%27%27ESPN.com%27%27&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.espn.com%2Fblog%2Fcollegebasketballnation%2Fpost%2F_%2Fid%2F116147%2Fwhich-players-injured-last-season-will-make-the-strongest-comebacks&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
